10.1016/j.jmp.2022.102669
Since the pioneering works of  Kahneman and Tversky (1979)  and  Tversky and Kahneman (1992) , probability weighting function has been a key element of Cumulative Prospect Theory and Rank-Dependent Utility Theory ( Quiggin, 1982, 1993; Schmeidler, 1989; Yaari, 1987 ). Several studies have proposed different expressions for these weighting functions and explore their motivations, properties, and consequences. Functions that appear more frequently in the literature are those given by  Goldstein and Einhorn (1987), Gul (1991), Karmarkar (1978), Tversky and Kahneman (1992), Wu and Gonzalez (1996)  and the two functions of  Prelec (1998) .

Since 1979, efforts have been made to build an axiomatization through which these weighting functions can be deduced. Some examples of such works are  Gonzalez and Wu (1999), Gul (1991), Prelec (1998) .

Axiomatization and the use of preference conditions is foundational in behavioral science. If one designs a practical experiment to identify the preference condition of a group of people, then axiomatization would allow us to find a weighting function to test against the collected data, scientifically.

For example, an insurance company is considering releasing a new job loss insurance product during a recession. The company can perform an experiment to identify the preference condition (consumer risk behavior) for a group of people, and determine the corresponding weighting functions. These weighting functions can then be used to quantitatively model the behavior of target customers.

Although it was not the focus of their paper,  Gonzalez and Wu (1999)  (appendix, Theorem 1) suggested a preference condition that was necessary and sufficient to get the  Goldstein–Einhorn Probability Weighting Functions

Since then, their theorem has been reported in several works,  Cavagnaro et al. (2013), Chudziak (2020), Diecidue et al. (2009), Fehr-Duda and Epper (2012)  and  Kilka and Weber (2001) .

The first contribution of the present paper is to show that the preference condition Gonzalez and Wu proposed leads to a wider set of solutions. Correctly identifying the set of solutions means we have more weighting functions at our disposal to calibrate the collected data. We find some new functional forms which can provide interesting properties that can be tested (e.g., allow for “anchor” points other than 0 and 1).

Following their axiomatic approach, by strengthening their preference condition, we deliver an axiomatization for the Goldstein–Einhorn functions. This is our second contribution. The new preference condition calls for new experimental testing.

The remainder of this article is organized as follows. Section  2  is a literature review. In Section  3 , we discuss the preference condition proposed by  Gonzalez and Wu (1999) . In Section  4  we present the solutions and provide some examples. In Section  5  we suggest a new axiomatization. In Section  6  we give our conclusions.

In this paper we will focus on the well-known Goldstein–Einhorn probability weighting functions defined by  (1) . It was introduced by  Goldstein and Einhorn (1987) , adding a linear coefficient in the formulation of  Karmarkar (1978, 1979) . Some variants of it were used by  Dierkes and Sejdiu (2019) ,  Kilka and Weber (2001)  and  Lattimore et al. (1992) .

Since then,  (1)  has been used across a wide spectrum. In an experimental study,  Gonzalez and Wu (1999)  tested many weighting functions and found that the two-parameter weighting functions suggested by  Goldstein and Einhorn (1987)  and  Prelec (1998)  performed very well, modeling two important psychological properties, discriminability and attractiveness. Recently,  Cavagnaro et al. (2013)  designed a simulation and choice experiment to discriminate among weighting functions. Again,  Goldstein and Einhorn (1987)  and  Prelec (1998)  were the best-fitting models.

Birnbaum and McIntosh (1996)  used  (1)  to study the violation of branch independence.  Birnbaumb et al. (2016)  examined binary gambles and three other kinds of violations: violations of complementary symmetry, violations of consequence monotonicity, and violations of first order stochastic dominance.  Kontek and Birnbaumb (2019)  researched violations of coalescing and stochastic dominance.

Young et al. (2011)  employed  (1)  in an experimental work to model attractiveness.  Fehr-Duda and Epper (2012)  applied the most common weighting functions, including  (1) , to explain important phenomena like the equity premium puzzle, the long-shot bias in betting markets, and households’ under-diversification and their willingness to buy small-scale insurance at exorbitant prices.  Bruhin et al. (2010)  chose  (1)  to model the distribution of risk-taking types in three different experimental data sets, two Swiss and one Chinese.  Chudziak (2020)  provided an alternative way to obtain  (1)  based on the concept of indifference prices.

In this section we discuss the  Preference Condition 1 , proposed in  Gonzalez and Wu (1999) . It is important to note that the authors developed an experimental work, therefore the analysis of the preference condition was not the focus of their paper.

The discussion is carried out under the Cumulative Prospect Theory (CPT) and the Rank-Dependent Utility Theory (RDUT). Generally, the two theories are different but following  Gonzalez and Wu (1999) , when we restrict to positive outcomes (gains), these theories coincide. Analogous conditions can be written for the case of negative outcomes (losses).

As a self-standing functional equation,  (11)  generally has more solutions than  (12) . It is in fact a mistake to claim that

The process terminates when we are able to confirm that the common solutions indeed satisfy the original functional equation. Otherwise, we have only to arrive at some necessary forms.

A functional equation which correctly characterizes the Goldstein–Einhorn weighting functions is the two-variable functional equation laid using the implication  (12)  in three variables

In this section we will solve  Problem 1  as an independent problem. We will establish and prove a series of propositions that not only solve the problem but also help us to construct some instructive particular solutions.

Proposition 3  is an alternative form of the solution of  (19) . However, a drawback is that not all periodic  
                         ϕ 
                       yield strictly increasing  
                         f 
                      . The form gives us a convenient means to construct differentiable examples beyond the Goldstein–Einhorn family. The drawback mentioned prevented us from obtaining the converse in  Proposition 3 .

In the previous section,  Propositions 2 and 3  presented us not only with the solution of  Problem 1  but also with two ways to build examples for the solutions.

In the examples provided in this section, we require a strictly increasing and continuous function  
                            f 
                         . Consequently, the respective weighting function  
                            w 
                          is also strictly increasing and continuous as required by CPT. In the  Appendix , we provide a general result for this requirement.

In this section we build two instructive examples. They are helpful in demonstrating the construction of the general solution, and in obtaining insights about psychological characteristics of the resulting weighting functions. The first example uses the periodic case of  
                            ϕ 
                          based on  Proposition 3 . The second example uses  Proposition 2  to build a piecewise linear solution, which is easy to analyze and provides us with numerous insights about the mathematical features of the solutions.

The family given by  (30)  gives us a lot of information about the behavior of the probability weighting functions. We can see how the parameters  
                               a 
                             and  
                               c 
                             influences the inverse s-shape, the fixed point, the attractiveness and the discriminability of the weighting functions. For more details about those important characteristics see for example  Abdellaoui et al. (2010), Diecidue et al. (2009), Gonzalez and Wu (1999), Webb and Zank (2011), Wu and Gonzalez (1996) .

From  (30) , if  
                               
                                  c 
                                  < 
                                  1 
                                  / 
                                  2 
                               
                             then  
                               f 
                             is concave and if  
                               
                                  c 
                                  > 
                                  1 
                                  / 
                                  2 
                               
                             it is convex. It influences the concave–convex form of  
                               w 
                            . More precisely, if  
                               
                                  c 
                                  < 
                                  1 
                                  / 
                                  2 
                               
                             then  
                               w 
                             is piecewise concave–convex and if  
                               
                                  c 
                                  > 
                                  1 
                                  / 
                                  2 
                               
                             then  
                               w 
                             is piecewise convex–concave. In addition, the values of  
                               a 
                             primarily determine the value of the fixed point of  
                               w 
                            .  Fig. 6  shows that conclusions. In the appendix, we provide a mathematical proof of these claims.

Since empirical works suggested that the weighting function is often concave–convex, so  
                               
                                  c 
                                  < 
                                  1 
                                  / 
                                  2 
                               
                             gets more attention.

With regard to the attractiveness and discriminability, the value of  
                               a 
                             primarily takes care of the attractiveness and the value of  
                               c 
                             deals with the discriminability. See  Fig. 7 .

It was mentioned earlier that our hypothesis of the weighting function  
                         w 
                       being continuous, strictly increasing and onto is transferred to  
                         f 
                       by  (10) . That assumption on  
                         f 
                       was entered in  Proposition 4 . Without that, the implication may fail to induce a functional equation.

Using  Preference Condition 2  and  Proposition 4 , we arrive at the following theorem.

Theorem 2 
                         
                            For non-negative two-outcome gambles under CPT,  
                            
                               Preference Condition  2  
                            is necessary and sufficient for  
                            
                               w 
                             
                            be the Goldstein–Einhorn Probability Weighting Functions.

For non-negative two-outcome gambles under CPT,  
                            
                               Preference Condition  2  
                            is necessary and sufficient for  
                            
                               w 
                             
                            be the Goldstein–Einhorn Probability Weighting Functions.

Analyzing the preference condition presented in  Gonzalez and Wu (1999)  ( Preference Condition 1 ), and its implied  Problem 1 , we find a large family of weighting functions where the  Goldstein–Einhorn Probability Weighting Functions  constitute a particular sub-family.

Also, we provide a new preference condition ( Preference Condition 2 ) which is necessary and sufficient to obtain the Goldstein–Einhorn Probability Weighting Functions.

Both preference conditions are difficult to test empirically because they rely on multiple antecedents. We hope future research uncovers easier testing conditions. For instance,  Prelec (1998)  proposed conditions which were difficult to test. Some time later,  Luce (2001)  presented a simpler condition to be used in practical experiments.

In many studies, it is very common to take  
                         
                            p 
                            = 
                            0 
                         
                       and  
                         
                            p 
                            = 
                            1 
                         
                       as reference points for diminishing sensitivity moving away from those endpoints. It will be interesting in future work to use the new functional families that emerge from Eq.  (11)  to test other reference points, such as  
                         
                            p 
                            = 
                            0 
                            . 
                            5 
                         
                      . The two competing ends seem to suggest that near  
                         
                            p 
                            = 
                            0 
                            . 
                            5 
                         
                      , the weighting function is more “linear”.

                         ϕ

                       is strictly increasing.

If
